Looking at this problem, I need to find the smallest $n$ such that for ANY distribution of answers, some pair of questions must have at least 57 students with matching answers (both correct or both incorrect). By contrapositive, I'll first find the maximum number of students possible such that the performance is NOT laughable, then add 1. ## Setting Up the Problem Each student has an answer vector $v \in \{0,1\}^{10}$, where 1 means correct and 0 means incorrect. For any pair of questions $(i,j)$, students can be classified into 4 groups based on $(v_i, v_j)$: - (0,0): both incorrect - (0,1): first incorrect, second correct - (1,0): first correct, second incorrect - (1,1): both correct The performance is **not laughable** if and only if: - For every pair of questions $(i,j)$: at most 56 students have pattern (0,0) - For every pair of questions $(i,j)$: at most 56 students have pattern (1,1) ## Key Observation: Complementary Vectors Consider two vectors $v$ and $\bar{v}$ (its bitwise complement). For any pair of questions $(i,j)$: - If $(v_i, v_j) = (0,0)$, then $(\bar{v}_i, \bar{v}_j) = (1,1)$ - If $(v_i, v_j) = (1,1)$, then $(\bar{v}_i, \bar{v}_j) = (0,0)$ - If $(v_i, v_j)$ is off-diagonal, so is $(\bar{v}_i, \bar{v}_j)$ (opposite off-diagonal) ## Partitioning Answer Vectors The $2^{10} = 1024$ possible answer vectors can be partitioned into **512 complementary pairs** $(v, \bar{v})$. For each complementary pair, let me assign: - $c_v$ students with vector $v$ - $c_{\bar{v}}$ students with vector $\bar{v}$ For any pair of questions $(i,j)$ where the vectors are diagonal (i.e., $v_i = v_j$): - Students with $v$ contribute to one diagonal group (either (0,0) or (1,1)) - Students with $\bar{v}$ contribute to the other diagonal group Therefore, we need: $c_v \leq 56$ AND $c_{\bar{v}} \leq 56$ This gives: $c_v + c_{\bar{v}} \leq 112$ ## Why This Bound is Tight For vectors with $v_i = v_j = 0$ (and thus $\bar{v}_i = \bar{v}_j = 1$): - If $v_i = v_j = 0$: the student with $v$ is in the (0,0) group for pair $(i,j)$, contributing to the (0,0) count - Its complement $\bar{v}$ would be in the (1,1) group for pair $(i,j)$, contributing to the (1,1) count Since this holds for every pair of questions and every complementary pair, we cannot exceed this bound. This is a hard constraint that cannot be violated simultaneously for all pairs of questions and all complementary pairs. ## Calculating Maximum With 512 complementary pairs, each supporting at most 112 students: $$n_{\max} = 512 \times 112 = 57,344$$ We can verify: $512 \times 112 = 512 \times 2 \times 56 = 2^9 \times 2 \times 56$ ## Conclusion Since we can have at most 57,344 students without the performance being laughable, adding just one more student guarantees the performance must be laughable. $$\boxed{57345}$$

Looking at this problem, I need to find the smallest $n$ where every possible answer configuration leads to a laughable performance. ## Setting up the problem For each student, I'll represent their answers as a binary string of length 10 (1 = correct, 0 = incorrect). Let $w_k$ denote the number of questions student $k$ answered correctly. For any pair of questions $(i,j)$, student $k$ falls into exactly one of four categories: (0,0), (0,1), (1,0), or (1,1). **The performance is NOT laughable** if and only if for every pair of questions $(i,j)$: - At most 56 students answered both questions the same way with both correct (11 pattern) - At most 56 students answered both questions the same way with both incorrect (00 pattern) ## Counting argument For each student $k$, the number of question pairs they answered both correctly is $\binom{w_k}{2}$, and the number answered both incorrectly is $\binom{10-w_k}{2}$. Summing over all students and all pairs: $$\sum_k \binom{w_k}{2} = \sum_{i<j} (\text{count of students with both 1 in positions } i,j)$$ There are $\binom{10}{2} = 45$ pairs of questions. If the performance is NOT laughable: $$\sum_k \binom{w_k}{2} \leq 45 \times 56 = 2520$$ $$\sum_k \binom{10-w_k}{2} \leq 45 \times 56 = 2520$$ ## Finding the minimum threshold For each student with $w_k$ correct answers: $$\binom{w_k}{2} + \binom{10-w_k}{2} = \frac{w_k(w_k-1)}{2} + \frac{(10-w_k)(9-w_k)}{2}$$ Expanding: $$= \frac{w_k^2 - w_k + 90 - 19w_k + w_k^2}{2} = w_k^2 - 10w_k + 45$$ This is minimized when $w_k = 5$, giving $25 - 50 + 45 = 20$. Therefore: $$\sum_k \left[\binom{w_k}{2} + \binom{10-w_k}{2}\right] \geq 20n$$ For NOT laughable: $$20n \leq 2520 + 2520 = 5040$$ $$n \leq 252$$ This means **if $n \geq 253$, the performance must be laughable**. ## Verifying $n = 252$ is achievable Can we construct 252 students with a non-laughable performance? Yes! Consider all $\binom{10}{5} = 252$ possible answer patterns with exactly 5 correct and 5 incorrect answers. For any pair of questions $(i,j)$: - The number of these patterns with both positions equal to 1 is $\binom{8}{3} = 56$ (choose 3 more positions from the remaining 8 to be correct) - The number with both positions equal to 0 is also $\binom{8}{3} = 56$ (choose 3 positions from the remaining 8 to be correct) With this configuration, every pair has exactly 56 students answering both the same way—this is **not laughable** since we need 57 or more for laughability. Therefore, with 252 students it's possible to avoid being laughable, but with 253 students it's impossible. **Final answer: 253**

Looking at this problem, I need to find the smallest $n$ such that for **any** assignment of answers, some pair of questions must have either 57 students answering both correctly or 57 answering both incorrectly. ## Setting Up the Problem I'll represent each student's answers as a binary vector in $\{0,1\}^{10}$. For each pair of questions $(i,j)$, each student falls into one of four categories based on their answers: - CC: both correct - CI: first correct, second incorrect - IC: first incorrect, second correct - II: both incorrect The performance is **not laughable** if and only if for every pair of questions, both CC and II groups have at most 56 students. ## Counting Argument Let $n_{ij}^{11}$ = number of students answering both questions $i,j$ correctly, and $n_{ij}^{00}$ = number answering both incorrectly. For a student with answer vector having Hamming weight $k$ (exactly $k$ correct answers): - This student contributes to $n_{ij}^{11}$ for $\binom{k}{2}$ pairs (choosing 2 positions from their $k$ correct answers) - This student contributes to $n_{ij}^{00}$ for $\binom{10-k}{2}$ pairs (choosing 2 positions from their $10-k$ incorrect answers) Summing over all students: $$\sum_{i<j} n_{ij}^{11} = \sum_{\text{students}} \binom{k_s}{2}$$ $$\sum_{i<j} n_{ij}^{00} = \sum_{\text{students}} \binom{10-k_s}{2}$$ ## Applying Constraints If the performance is not laughable, then $n_{ij}^{11} \leq 56$ and $n_{ij}^{00} \leq 56$ for all $\binom{10}{2} = 45$ pairs. Therefore: $$\sum_{\text{students}} \binom{k_s}{2} \leq 45 \times 56 = 2520$$ $$\sum_{\text{students}} \binom{10-k_s}{2} \leq 45 \times 56 = 2520$$ Adding these inequalities: $$\sum_{\text{students}} \left[\binom{k_s}{2} + \binom{10-k_s}{2}\right] \leq 5040$$ ## Key Calculation For each $k$, I'll compute: $$\binom{k}{2} + \binom{10-k}{2} = \frac{k(k-1)}{2} + \frac{(10-k)(9-k)}{2}$$ $$= \frac{k^2 - k + 90 - 19k + k^2}{2} = \frac{2k^2 - 20k + 90}{2}$$ $$= k^2 - 10k + 45 = (k-5)^2 + 20 \geq 20$$ The minimum value is 20, achieved when $k = 5$. Looking at this problem, I need to find the smallest $n$ such that performance is *necessarily* laughable, which means I should find the maximum number of students with non-laughable performance, then add 1. ## Understanding Non-Laughable Performance Performance is **not laughable** if and only if for **every** pair of questions $(i,j)$: - At most 56 students answered both correctly (pattern 1,1) - At most 56 students answered both incorrectly (pattern 0,0) ## Counting Matching Pairs For each student, I'll count how many pairs of questions they answered the same way (both correct or both incorrect). If a student answers $w$ questions correctly, they have matching answers on: - $\binom{w}{2}$ pairs where both answers are 1 - $\binom{10-w}{2}$ pairs where both answers are 0 Total matching pairs: $$\binom{w}{2} + \binom{10-w}{2} = \frac{w(w-1)}{2} + \frac{(10-w)(9-w)}{2}$$ $$= \frac{w^2 - w + 100 - 20w + w^2 - 10 + w}{2} = w^2 - 10w + 45$$ I can rewrite this as: $$w^2 - 10w + 45 = (w-5)^2 + 20$$ The minimum value is **20** (achieved when $w = 5$). ## Applying the Upper Bound Let's count the total "student-question pair matches" in two ways. **By pairs:** There are $\binom{10}{2} = 45$ pairs of questions. For non-laughable performance, each pair contributes at most $56 + 56 = 112$ matches (at most 56 of type 1,1 and at most 56 of type 0,0). Total matches: $\sum_{i<j}(\text{matches on pair } (i,j)) \leq 45 \times 112 = 5040$ **By students:** If we have $n$ students with weights $w_1, \ldots, w_n$: $$\sum_{k=1}^n \left[(w_k-5)^2 + 20\right] \leq 5040$$ $$\sum_{k=1}^n (w_k-5)^2 + 20n \leq 5040$$ Since $(w_k-5)^2 \geq 0$ for all $k$: $$20n \leq 5040$$ $$n \leq 252$$ ## Verifying the Bound is Tight Can we achieve exactly $n = 252$ with non-laughable performance? Consider all 10-bit strings with **exactly 5 ones**. There are $\binom{10}{5} = 252$ such strings. For any pair of positions $(i,j)$: - Strings with both positions = 1: choose these 2 positions, then 3 more from remaining 8 → $\binom{8}{3} = 56$ ✓ - Strings with both positions = 0: choose 5 positions from remaining 8 → $\binom{8}{5} = 56$ ✓ - Other patterns: $\binom{8}{4} = 70$ each Total: $56 + 56 + 70 + 70 = 252$ ✓ Using all $\binom{10}{5} = 252$ strings of weight exactly 5 gives a non-laughable configuration where each pair achieves the maximum allowed (exactly 56 for each matching type). ## Conclusion The maximum number of students with non-laughable performance is exactly 252. Therefore, with 253 students, the performance is necessarily laughable. **Final answer: 253**

Looking at this problem, I need to find the minimum $n$ such that no matter how students answer the questions, the performance must be laughable. ## Setting up the problem Let me represent each student's answers as a binary vector in $\{0,1\}^{10}$. For any pair of questions $(i,j)$, I can partition the $n$ students into four groups: - **CC**: both answers correct - **II**: both answers incorrect - **CI**: correct on $i$, incorrect on $j$ - **IC**: incorrect on $i$, correct on $j$ The performance is laughable if for some pair of questions, either CC or II has at least 57 students. The performance is **not laughable** if for every pair of questions, both CC and II have at most 56 students. ## Finding the maximum $n$ without laughability I want to find the maximum $n$ such that we **can** avoid being laughable, then the answer will be $n+1$. For a student with answer vector having $k$ ones and $10-k$ zeros: - This vector contributes to CC counts for $\binom{k}{2}$ pairs (pairs where both answers are correct) - This vector contributes to II counts for $\binom{10-k}{2}$ pairs (pairs where both answers are incorrect) Let $m_v$ denote the number of students with vector $v$. Summing over all pairs of questions: $$\sum_{\text{all pairs}} (\text{CC count}) = \sum_v m_v \binom{k_v}{2}$$ $$\sum_{\text{all pairs}} (\text{II count}) = \sum_v m_v \binom{10-k_v}{2}$$ Since each pair can have at most 56 students in CC and 56 in II, and there are $\binom{10}{2} = 45$ pairs: $$\sum_v m_v \binom{k_v}{2} \leq 45 \times 56 = 2520$$ $$\sum_v m_v \binom{10-k_v}{2} \leq 45 \times 56 = 2520$$ Adding these inequalities: $$\sum_v m_v \left(\binom{k_v}{2} + \binom{10-k_v}{2}\right) \leq 5040$$ ## Minimizing the coefficient Let me compute $\binom{k}{2} + \binom{10-k}{2}$: $$\binom{k}{2} + \binom{10-k}{2} = \frac{k(k-1)}{2} + \frac{(10-k)(9-k)}{2}$$ $$= \frac{k^2 - k + 90 - 19k + k^2}{2} = \frac{2k^2 - 20k + 90}{2}$$ $$= k^2 - 10k + 45 = (k-5)^2 + 20$$ This is minimized when $k = 5$, giving a minimum value of $\boxed{20}$. Therefore: $$\sum_v m_v \cdot 20 \leq 5040 \implies n = \sum_v m_v \leq 252$$ ## Verifying $n = 252$ is achievable Let me check if we can actually achieve $n = 252$ without laughability. Consider using exactly one student for each of the $\binom{10}{5} = 252$ possible vectors with exactly 5 ones. For any pair of questions $(i,j)$: - **CC count** = vectors with 1s at both positions = $\binom{8}{3} = 56$ ✓ - **II count** = vectors with 0s at both positions = $\binom{8}{5} = 56$ ✓ Perfect! Adding any additional student (with any answer vector) would increase at least one CC or II count beyond 56, making the performance laughable. ## Conclusion The maximum value of $n$ for which we can avoid laughability is 252. Looking at this problem, I need to find the minimum $n$ such that any distribution of student answers must result in a "laughable" performance. ## Understanding the Constraints For any pair of questions $(i,j)$, each student falls into one of four response categories: - Both correct (1,1) - First correct, second wrong (1,0) - First wrong, second correct (0,1) - Both wrong (0,0) The performance is laughable if for some pair, either: - ≥57 students answered both the same way with both correct, OR - ≥57 students answered both the same way with both incorrect To find the minimum $n$ that **guarantees** being laughable, I need to find the maximum $n$ that can **avoid** being laughable, then add 1. ## Setting up the Counting Argument Let $n_v$ denote the number of students with response pattern $v \in \{0,1\}^{10}$. To avoid being laughable, for each of the $\binom{10}{2} = 45$ pairs of questions $(i,j)$: - $\sum_{v: v_i=1, v_j=1} n_v \leq 56$ - $\sum_{v: v_i=0, v_j=0} n_v \leq 56$ ## Deriving an Upper Bound For a pattern $v$, let $k_v$ denote the number of questions answered correctly. The number of pairs where both coordinates equal 1 is $\binom{k_v}{2}$. The number of pairs where both coordinates equal 0 is $\binom{10-k_v}{2}$. Summing the first constraint over all pairs: $$\sum_v n_v \binom{k_v}{2} \leq 56 \times 45 = 2520$$ Summing the second constraint over all pairs: $$\sum_v n_v \binom{10-k_v}{2} \leq 56 \times 45 = 2520$$ Adding these inequalities: $$\sum_v n_v \left[\binom{k_v}{2} + \binom{10-k_v}{2}\right] \leq 5040$$ Let me compute $f(k) = \binom{k}{2} + \binom{10-k}{2}$: $$f(k) = \frac{k(k-1)}{2} + \frac{(10-k)(9-k)}{2} = \frac{k^2 - k + 90 - 19k + k^2}{2} = k^2 - 10k + 45$$ Completing the square: $$f(k) = (k-5)^2 + 20$$ This is minimized at $k=5$ with $f(5) = 20$. Since $f(k) \geq 20$ for all $k$: $$\sum_v n_v \cdot 20 \leq 5040$$ $$n \leq 252$$ ## Achieving Equality Consider using **only patterns with exactly 5 correct answers**. There are $\binom{10}{5} = 252$ such patterns. For any pair of questions $(i,j)$: - Patterns with both correct: need both $i,j$ in the 5 correct positions = $\binom{8}{3} = 56$ ✓ - Patterns with both incorrect: need both $i,j$ in the 5 incorrect positions = $\binom{8}{3} = 56$ ✓ With one student per such pattern, we have exactly $n = 252$ students and each pair of questions has exactly 56 students in both the (1,1) and (0,0) categories. Looking at this problem, I need to find the smallest $n$ such that no matter how students answer, there must exist some pair of questions where at least 57 students answered both the same way (either both correct or both incorrect). ## Setting Up the Problem For each student, let me represent their answers as a binary string of length 10. For any pair of questions $(i,j)$, I can categorize each student based on their answers: - **CC**: answered both questions correctly - **II**: answered both questions incorrectly - **CI**: correct on question $i$, incorrect on question $j$ - **IC**: incorrect on question $i$, correct on question $j$ Let $N_{ij}^{CC}$ and $N_{ij}^{II}$ denote the number of students in each category for pair $(i,j)$. The performance is **laughable** if for some pair $(i,j)$: either $N_{ij}^{CC} \geq 57$ or $N_{ij}^{II} \geq 57$. ## Counting Argument The key observation: For each student with answer pattern having $m$ correct answers, how many question pairs have matching answers (both correct or both incorrect)? The answer is: $\binom{m}{2} + \binom{10-m}{2}$ pairs. Let me minimize this expression: $$f(m) = \binom{m}{2} + \binom{10-m}{2} = \frac{m(m-1)}{2} + \frac{(10-m)(9-m)}{2}$$ $$= \frac{m^2 - m + 100 - 20m + m^2 - 10 + m}{2} = m^2 - 10m + 45 = (m-5)^2 + 20$$ The minimum value is **20**, achieved when $m = 5$ (exactly 5 correct answers). Therefore: $$\sum_{i<j} (N_{ij}^{CC} + N_{ij}^{II}) \geq 20n$$ ## The Key Constraint To avoid laughability, I need: $N_{ij}^{CC} \leq 56$ AND $N_{ij}^{II} \leq 56$ for all pairs. This means: $N_{ij}^{CC} + N_{ij}^{II} \leq 112$ for each pair. Note: If $N_{ij}^{CC} + N_{ij}^{II} \geq 113$, then by pigeonhole principle, at least one of them must be $\geq 57$, making the performance laughable. Since there are $\binom{10}{2} = 45$ pairs: $$\sum_{i<j} (N_{ij}^{CC} + N_{ij}^{II}) \leq 45 \times 112 = 5040$$ ## Finding the Answer Combining the inequalities: $$20n \leq \sum_{i<j} (N_{ij}^{CC} + N_{ij}^{II}) \leq 5040$$ Therefore: $n \leq 252$ ## Verification With exactly $n = 252$, this maximum is achievable! Use all $\binom{10}{5} = 252$ possible answer patterns where exactly 5 questions are answered correctly. For any pair of questions $(i,j)$: - $N_{ij}^{CC} = \binom{8}{3} = 56$ (patterns containing both $i$ and $j$) - $N_{ij}^{II} = \binom{8}{5} = 56$ (patterns containing neither $i$ nor $j$) So with $n = 252$, it's possible to have a non-laughable configuration. With $n = 253$: $$\sum_{i<j} (N_{ij}^{CC} + N_{ij}^{II}) \geq 20 \times 253 = 5060 > 5040$$ By pigeonhole principle, at least one pair must have $N_{ij}^{CC} + N_{ij}^{II} \geq 113$, which forces either $N_{ij}^{CC} \geq 57$ or $N_{ij}^{II} \geq 57$, making the performance laughable. **Final answer: 253**

Looking at this problem, I need to find the smallest $n$ such that for ANY distribution of answers, the performance is laughable. This is equivalent to finding the maximum $n$ where it's POSSIBLE to avoid being laughable, then adding 1. ## Setting up the constraints For any pair of questions $(i,j)$, let me define: - $a_{ij}$ = number of students answering both correctly - $b_{ij}$ = number of students answering both incorrectly To avoid being laughable, we need: - $a_{ij} \leq 56$ for all pairs $(i,j)$ - $b_{ij} \leq 56$ for all pairs $(i,j)$ There are $\binom{10}{2} = 45$ pairs of questions. ## Counting argument For each student $s$, let $k_s$ be the number of questions they answer correctly. - Each student with $k_s$ correct answers contributes to $\binom{k_s}{2}$ pairs (the pairs of correct answers) - Each student with $10-k_s$ incorrect answers contributes to $\binom{10-k_s}{2}$ pairs (the pairs of incorrect answers) Therefore: $$\sum_{i<j} a_{ij} = \sum_{s=1}^{n} \binom{k_s}{2}$$ $$\sum_{i<j} b_{ij} = \sum_{s=1}^{n} \binom{10-k_s}{2}$$ To avoid being laughable: $$\sum_{s=1}^{n} \binom{k_s}{2} \leq 45 \times 56 = 2520$$ $$\sum_{s=1}^{n} \binom{10-k_s}{2} \leq 45 \times 56 = 2520$$ Adding these inequalities: $$\sum_{s=1}^{n} \left[\binom{k_s}{2} + \binom{10-k_s}{2}\right] \leq 5040$$ ## Finding the minimum cost per student For any $k$: $$\binom{k}{2} + \binom{10-k}{2} = \frac{k(k-1)}{2} + \frac{(10-k)(9-k)}{2}$$ $$= \frac{k^2 - k + 90 - 19k + k^2}{2} = k^2 - 10k + 45 = (k-5)^2 + 20$$ The minimum value is $20$, achieved when $k = 5$. Therefore: $$\sum_{s=1}^{n} [(k_s-5)^2 + 20] \leq 5040$$ $$20n + \sum_{s=1}^{n} (k_s-5)^2 \leq 5040$$ Since $(k_s - 5)^2 \geq 0$: $$20n \leq 5040$$ $$n \leq 252$$ ## Verifying $n = 252$ is achievable Equality holds when all students answer exactly $k_s = 5$ questions correctly. Consider assigning each of the $\binom{10}{5} = 252$ possible 5-element subsets to exactly one student. For any pair of questions $(i,j)$: - **Both correct**: Number of 5-element subsets containing both $i$ and $j$ = $\binom{8}{3} = 56$ ✓ - **Both incorrect**: Number of 5-element subsets containing neither $i$ nor $j$ = $\binom{8}{5} = 56$ ✓ So with $n = 252$, we can achieve exactly 56 for each constraint for every pair—this is NOT laughable. ## When must it be laughable? With $n = 253$ students, adding any student with answer pattern requires: $$20(253) = 5060 > 5040$$ This violates our constraint, making it impossible to avoid being laughable. **Final answer: 253**

The student contributes to homogeneous pairs (both same answer) as follows: - Contributes to $a_{ij}$ for $\binom{k_s}{2}$ pairs (both questions answered correctly) - Contributes to $d_{ij}$ for $\binom{10-k_s}{2}$ pairs (both questions answered incorrectly) The total number of homogeneous pairs for student $s$ is: $$\binom{k_s}{2} + \binom{10-k_s}{2} = \frac{k_s(k_s-1) + (10-k_s)(9-k_s)}{2} = k_s^2 - 10k_s + 45 = (k_s-5)^2 + 20$$ This is **minimized when $k_s = 5$**, giving exactly 20 homogeneous pairs. ## Double-counting argument Counting the sum of all homogeneous pair contributions: $$\sum_s \left[\binom{k_s}{2} + \binom{10-k_s}{2}\right] = \sum_{(i,j)} (a_{ij} + d_{ij})$$ The left side: Each of $n$ students contributes at least 20, so $$\text{LHS} \geq 20n$$ The right side: For the performance to not be laughable, $a_{ij} \leq 56$ and $d_{ij} \leq 56$, so: $$a_{ij} + d_{ij} \leq 112 \text{ for each pair}$$ With 45 pairs: $$\text{RHS} \leq 45 \times 112 = 5040$$ Therefore: $20n \leq 5040$, which gives $n \leq 252$. ## Verifying equality is achievable Consider all $\binom{10}{5} = 252$ binary patterns with exactly 5 ones and 5 zeros. For any pair of positions $(i,j)$: - **Patterns with both 1s at positions $i,j$:** Choose 3 more positions from remaining 8 for the remaining ones: $\binom{8}{3} = 56$ patterns - **Patterns with both 0s at positions $i,j$:** Choose 5 positions from remaining 8 for ones: $\binom{8}{5} = 56$ patterns If we assign one student to each of these 252 patterns, then for every pair: $$a_{ij} = 56 \text{ and } d_{ij} = 56$$ The performance is **not laughable** with 252 students. ## Adding the 253rd student When we add a 253rd student, by the pigeonhole principle, their pattern cannot have exactly 5 ones (all such patterns are taken). If they have $k \neq 5$ ones, then: $$\binom{k}{2} + \binom{10-k}{2} \geq 21 > 20$$ Since the 253rd student must contribute to some homogeneous pairs, and every pair already has either $a_{ij} = 56$ or $d_{ij} = 56$ (and in fact both equal 56), the 253rd student must increase at least one count from 56 to 57. Therefore, the performance becomes laughable. **Final answer: 253**

Looking at this problem, I need to find the minimum $n$ such that for ANY distribution of student answers, there must exist some pair of questions where at least 57 students answered both the same way (either both correctly or both incorrectly). ## Setting up the problem For each of the 10 questions, a student's answers can be represented as a binary string. For any pair of questions $(i,j)$, each student falls into one of four categories: - Both correct (1,1) - First correct, second incorrect (1,0) - First incorrect, second correct (0,1) - Both incorrect (0,0) A performance is **not laughable** if for every pair of questions, both the (1,1) count and the (0,0) count are at most 56. ## Counting argument Let me count how many pairs contribute (1,1) or (0,0) counts for each answer pattern. For a pattern with exactly $k$ correct answers out of 10: - Number of (1,1) pairs: $\binom{k}{2}$ - Number of (0,0) pairs: $\binom{10-k}{2}$ The total contribution is: $$\binom{k}{2} + \binom{10-k}{2} = \frac{k(k-1)}{2} + \frac{(10-k)(9-k)}{2}$$ $$= \frac{k^2 - k + 90 - 19k + k^2}{2} = k^2 - 10k + 45 = (k-5)^2 + 20$$ This is minimized when $k=5$, giving a value of **20**. ## Bounding the total number of students There are $\binom{10}{2} = 45$ pairs of questions. Each constraint pair can have at most 56 students in (1,1) and at most 56 students in (0,0), so: $$\sum_{\text{all pairs}} (\text{(1,1) count} + \text{(0,0) count}) \leq 45 \times 2 \times 56 = 5040$$ If we have $n$ students and they use answer patterns $v$ with multiplicities $m_v$, then: $$\sum_{v} m_v \cdot [(k_v-5)^2 + 20] \leq 5040$$ where $k_v$ is the number of correct answers in pattern $v$. Since $(k_v-5)^2 \geq 0$: $$20n \leq 5040$$ $$n \leq 252$$ ## Achieving the bound with 252 students There are exactly $\binom{10}{5} = 252$ patterns with precisely 5 correct answers. For any such pattern and any pair of questions $(i,j)$: - Patterns with 1 at both positions: $\binom{8}{3} = 56$ (fix positions $i,j$ as 1, choose 3 more from 8 remaining) - Patterns with 0 at both positions: $\binom{8}{5} = 56$ (fix positions $i,j$ as 0, choose 5 from 8 remaining) **If we assign each of the 252 patterns with exactly 5 correct answers to exactly one student**, then for every pair of questions, exactly 56 students answered both correctly and exactly 56 answered both incorrectly. This is the maximum allowed, so the performance is **not laughable**. ## Why 253 students guarantee laughability With 253 students, we must assign at least two students the same pattern, or assign at least one student a pattern with $k \neq 5$ correct answers. For any pattern $w$, the number of pairs where both answers match (both 0 or both 1) is at least: $$(k-5)^2 + 20 \geq 20$$ So adding any 253rd student increases either some (1,1) count or some (0,0) count. Since each is already at 56 (the maximum), adding the 253rd student must push at least one count to 57. Therefore, with $n=253$, the performance is necessarily laughable. **Final answer: 253**

response
Looking at this problem, I need to find the minimum $n$ such that for any distribution of answers among $n$ students, there must exist some pair of questions where at least 57 students answered both the same way (both correct or both incorrect). **Setting up the problem using Hamming weights:** For each student, I can represent their answers as a binary vector in $\{0,1\}^{10}$ where 1 = correct and 0 = incorrect. Let $w(v)$ denote the Hamming weight (number of correct answers) of a student's vector. For any binary vector $v$ with Hamming weight $k$: - The vector contributes to **(1,1) pairs**: $\binom{k}{2}$ pairs where both positions have 1 - The vector contributes to **(0,0) pairs**: $\binom{10-k}{2}$ pairs where both positions have 0 **Finding constraints on total student counts:** Let $n_k$ be the number of students with Hamming weight $k$. Summing over all 10 positions: $$\text{Total (1,1) count across all } \binom{10}{2}=45 \text{ pairs} = \sum_{k=0}^{10} n_k \binom{k}{2}$$ $$\text{Total (0,0) count across all 45 pairs} = \sum_{k=0}^{10} n_k \binom{10-k}{2}$$ For the performance to **NOT be laughable**, we need: - Each pair has fewer than 57 students with (1,1), so the total is $\leq 45 \times 56 = 2520$ - Each pair has fewer than 57 students with (0,0), so the total is $\leq 45 \times 56 = 2520$ **Maximizing students without being laughable:** By symmetry, the optimal solution should have $n_k = n_{10-k}$ for all $k$. This way both constraints are equivalent. To maximize $\sum n_k$ subject to $\sum n_k \binom{k}{2} \leq 2520$, I should use students with the smallest coefficients $\binom{k}{2}$: - $k=0,1$: $\binom{k}{2} = 0$ - $k=1,9$: Second constraint gives $\binom{9}{2} = 36$ or $\binom{10}{2} = 45$ **Computing the maximum for weight 1 and 9:** Using only weights 1 and 9 with symmetry $n_1 = n_9$: - Constraint: $36n_1 \leq 2520$ - Therefore: $n_1 \leq 70$ - Total students: $n = 2 \times 70 = 140$ **Verifying this works:** With 7 copies of each of the 10 weight-1 vectors and 7 copies of each of the 10 weight-9 vectors: For any pair $(i,j)$: - Weight-1 students with (0,0): Those with their single 1 at positions not in $\{i,j\}$, giving 8 positions × 7 copies = **56 students** - Weight-9 students with (1,1): Those with their single 0 at positions not in $\{i,j\}$, giving 8 positions × 7 copies = **56 students** Every pair has exactly 56 students of each type. ✓ **Adding the 141st student:** When we add any 141st student: - If weight 1 or 9: They contribute to 36 pairs' (0,0) or (1,1) counts respectively, pushing those pairs from 56 to **57** - If weight 2-8: Total (1,1) count becomes $> 2520$, so by pigeonhole some pair exceeds 56 In all cases, the performance becomes laughable. **Final answer: 141**

"""Deterministic verifier — contributor_03 / problem_01.
Laughable Performance in Math Competition. Hand-authored (Claude Code, autonomous; via @yulinl2).
Exact integer final-answer match against the author oracle.
"""

import re
from fractions import Fraction

_MARKERS = [
 r"final\s+answer\s*[:=]?", r"the\s+(?:exact\s+)?answer\s+is\s*[:=]?",
 r"answer\s*[:=]", r"the\s+rate\s+is\s*[:=]?", r"therefore[,:]?",
 r"thus[,:]?", r"hence[,:]?",
]
_MARKER_RE = re.compile("|".join(_MARKERS), re.IGNORECASE)

def _boxed_inner(s):
 """Brace-balanced contents of every \\boxed{...} (handles nested \\frac)."""
 out, i = [], 0
 while True:
 j = s.find("boxed", i)
 if j < 0:
 break
 k = s.find("{", j)
 if k < 0:
 break
 depth, m = 0, k
 while m < len(s):
 if s[m] == "{":
 depth += 1
 elif s[m] == "}":
 depth -= 1
 if depth == 0:
 break
 m += 1
 out.append(s[k + 1:m])
 i = m + 1
 return out

def _normalize(s):
 """LaTeX -> plain so rationals survive: \\frac{a}{b} -> a/b, keep %.
 (c09 fractions are always integer/integer, so the flat a/b form is exact;
 a compound numerator would need parens, not present here.)"""
 # capture an optional leading minus OUTSIDE the frac (-\frac{a}{b} -> -a/b):
 # a detached "-" would otherwise be split off and the component read positive.
 s = re.sub(r"(-?)\s*\\[dt]?frac\s*\{([^{}]*)\}\s*\{([^{}]*)\}", r" \1\2/\3 ", s)
 s = re.sub(r"\\(left|right|!|,|;|:|quad|qquad)", " ", s)
 s = re.sub(r"\\text\s*\{[^{}]*\}", " ", s)
 s = s.replace(r"\%", "%").replace(r"\$", " ").replace("$", " ")
 s = s.replace("{,}", "") # 225{,}000 -> 225000
 s = s.replace("\\", " ").replace("{", " ").replace("}", " ")
 # thousands separator ONLY when strictly adjacent (1,250 -> 1250); never join
 # across a space (a vector's "1907, 379" must stay two numbers).
 s = re.sub(r"(\d),(\d{3})(?!\d)", r"\1\2", s)
 return s

# token: percent | fraction a/b | decimal | integer (left-to-right, non-overlap)
_TOK = re.compile(
 r"(?P<pct>-?\d+(?:\.\d+)?)\s*%"
 r"|(?P<n>-?\d+)\s*/\s*(?P<d>-?\d+)"
 r"|(?P<dec>-?\d+\.\d+)"
 r"|(?P<int>-?\d+)")

def _rationals(zone):
 """Ordered list of Fraction values committed in `zone` (already a string)."""
 out = []
 for m in _TOK.finditer(_normalize(zone)):
 try:
 if m.group("pct") is not None:
 out.append(Fraction(m.group("pct")) / 100)
 elif m.group("n") is not None:
 d = int(m.group("d"))
 if d == 0:
 continue
 out.append(Fraction(int(m.group("n")), d))
 elif m.group("dec") is not None:
 out.append(Fraction(m.group("dec")))
 else:
 out.append(Fraction(int(m.group("int"))))
 except (ValueError, ZeroDivisionError):
 continue
 return out

def _final_zone(response):
 """The text of the model's committed final answer (most -> least reliable):
 boxed contents; else the last marker line's tail; else the last line."""
 if not response or not response.strip:
 return ""
 boxed = _boxed_inner(response)
 if boxed:
 return " ".join(boxed)
 lines = [ln for ln in response.splitlines if ln.strip]
 # last marker line that actually carries a number (weak discourse markers
 # like "therefore/thus/hence" otherwise hijack the zone to a number-less
 # trailing comment, burying the real commit on the line above).
 for ln in reversed(lines):
 m = _MARKER_RE.search(ln)
 if m and any(c.isdigit for c in ln[m.start:]):
 return ln[m.start:]
 # else the last line that carries a number
 for ln in reversed(lines):
 if any(c.isdigit for c in ln):
 return ln
 return lines[-1] if lines else ""

def _committed_rational(zone):
 """The single COMMITTED final rational in `zone`: the value after the LAST
 '=' if one is present (so an arithmetic line like '105 - 105 = 0' commits 0,
 NOT the intermediate 105 — the loose 'oracle appears anywhere' false-positive
 the cold-read found), else the last rational. Returns (value|None, all)."""
 cands = _rationals(zone)
 if not cands:
 return None, []
 norm = _normalize(zone)
 if "=" in norm:
 tail = _rationals(norm.rsplit("=", 1)[1])
 if tail:
 return tail[-1], cands
 # no '=': the committed value is the FIRST rational (right after the marker /
 # at the head of the line) — a trailing parenthetical like "0 (note 105)" or
 # "253 (> 200)" must not steal the verdict.
 return cands[0], cands

def match_exact(response, oracle):
 """oracle: Fraction. The COMMITTED final must equal it EXACTLY (no tolerance
 — rounded-only decimals are insufficient per the gradingscheme). Grades the
 committed value, not any number in the zone (no substring/intermediate pass)."""
 committed, cands = _committed_rational(_final_zone(response))
 if committed is None:
 return False, "no rational final answer could be extracted"
 if committed == oracle:
 return True, "committed %s == oracle %s" % (committed, oracle)
 return False, "committed %s != oracle %s (cands %s)" % (
 committed, oracle, [str(c) for c in cands])

def match_vector(response, oracle):
 """oracle: list[Fraction]. Every component must appear in the final zone."""
 cands = set(_rationals(_final_zone(response)))
 missing = [str(o) for o in oracle if o not in cands]
 if not missing:
 return True, "all %d components present" % len(oracle)
 return False, "missing/incorrect components: %s" % missing

def match_rate(response, oracle):
 """oracle: Fraction rate (e.g. 39/125). Accept the exact fraction, the
 decimal (tol 1e-4, the percent form is inherently rounded), or the percent
 (bare integer like 31.2 read as 31.2%)."""
 committed, cands = _committed_rational(_final_zone(response))
 if committed is None:
 return False, "no rational final answer could be extracted"
 r = oracle
 rf = float(r)
 cf = float(committed)
 if committed == r:
 return True, "exact %s == oracle %s" % (committed, r)
 if abs(cf - rf) < 1e-4:
 return True, "decimal %s ~= oracle %s" % (committed, r)
 if abs(cf - rf * 100) < 1e-2:
 return True, "percent %s ~= oracle %s%%" % (committed, float(r * 100))
 return False, "committed %s != oracle %s (=%.6g; cands %s)" % (
 committed, r, rf, [str(c) for c in cands])


def grade(response: str, context: dict) -> dict:
 """Laughable Performance in Math Competition

 Oracle: Fraction(253) (author answerkey; frontier-sweep consensus matches). Exact
 integer final-answer match — intermediate numbers in the derivation are
 ignored (only the committed final zone is graded)."""
 ok, reason = match_exact(response, Fraction(253))
 return {"pass": bool(ok), "score": 1.0 if ok else 0.0, "notes": reason}

💭 What's promising
  • Lovely benchmark target: a double-counting + pigeonhole argument that frontier models routinely fumble on the discrete-rounding step.
  • Single-integer deliverable maps perfectly onto an exact-match verifier — no grading ambiguity once the oracle is pinned down.
  • The per-student lower bound (each student forces ≥ 20 matched pairs via the $c_i=5$ minimum) is set up correctly and cleanly.
❓ Open questions to think through
  • The sidecar is still the untouched template — no sample model responses, so we can't yet confirm this stumps frontier models in 3/3 runs.
  • The stated derivation supports n = 127, not 253: $\lceil 4n/9\rceil \ge 57$ gives $4n/9 > 56 \Rightarrow n > 126$. The leap to 253 is hand-waved ('looking closer at the exact distribution... robustly force') and isn't a real argument.
  • 253 may well be the correct answer to the intended (harder) problem, but the averaging bound as written is too weak to prove it — the oracle and its proof are currently inconsistent.

#02

Tenuous Function Minimal Value

Polished optimization problem with a correct-looking oracle (136) — but it's a verbatim HMMT competition problem, so contamination is the headline risk.

📄 Submission snapshot — parsed source evidence (private bank, unreleased; do not redistribute)
An integer-valued function $f$ is called tenuous if $f(x)+f(y)>x^{2}$ for all positive integers $x$ and $y$.ambiguity: The named term 'tenuous' and this exact phrasing are the HMMT 2010 original — strong memorization hook. Also note the bound depends only on x: the y-quantifier effectively only constrains the global minimum. Solvers may stumble on the asymmetry. Let $g$ be a tenuous function such that $g(1)+g(2)+\cdots+g(20)$ is as small as possiblesuggestion: Reskinning candidate: changing the sum range and the queried index (14) together, plus the threshold, is the cheapest way to break verbatim recall while preserving the reasoning.. Compute the minimum possible value for $g(14)$.strong: Clean single-integer deliverable — ideal for an exact-match verifier.

Hover (desktop) or tap (touch) a highlighted span for reviewer's comment. Kind: strong suggestion ambiguity nit missing

Oracle (answer key) — the contributor's reference solution this review is checked against.

The minimum possible value for $g(14)$ is 136. \textbf{Solution:} Let $m = \min_{y \in \mathbb{Z}^+} g(y)$ be the global minimum of the function $g$. Since $g$ is a tenuous function, for any positive integers $x$ and $y$, we are given: $$ g(x) + g(y) > x^2 $$ Because $g$ is an integer-valued function, the strict inequality can be written as: $$ g(x) + g(y) \ge x^2 + 1 $$ By choosing $y$ such that $g(y) = m$, we obtain: $$ g(x) + m \ge x^2 + 1 \implies g(x) \ge x^2 + 1 - m $$ By the definition of $m$, it is also always true that $g(x) \ge m$. Therefore, for all positive integers $x$: $$ g(x) \ge \max(m, \ x^2 + 1 - m) $$ We want to minimize the sum $\sum_{n=1}^{20} g(n)$. To achieve the minimum possible sum, $g(n)$ must reach its lower bound for all $1 \le n \le 20$. Let $S(m)$ be this minimum possible sum: $$ S(m) = \sum_{n=1}^{20} \max(m, \ n^2 + 1 - m) $$ We need to find the integer $m$ that minimizes $S(m)$. Let's analyze how the terms change as $m$ increases. For a fixed $n$, let $f_n(m) = \max(m, n^2 + 1 - m)$. If we increase $m$ by 1 (from $m$ to $m+1$): - $f_n(m)$ increases by 1 if $m > n^2 + 1 - m$, which means $2m > n^2 + 1$. - $f_n(m)$ decreases by 1 if $m \le n^2 + 1 - m$, which means $2m \le n^2 + 1$. To minimize $S(m)$, the number of terms that increase must not exceed the number of terms that decrease. Since there are 20 terms in total, we can afford at most 10 terms to increase. This means the condition $2m > n^2 + 1$ can hold for at most the first 10 values of $n$ ($n=1, 2, \dots, 10$). For $n = 11$, we must have: $$ 2m \le 11^2 + 1 = 122 \implies m \le 61 $$ At the same time, we need at least 10 terms to increase to stop the sum from decreasing when $m$ goes backwards. This requires the condition to hold for $n=10$: $$ 2m > 10^2 + 1 = 101 \implies m \ge 51 $$ Therefore, $S(m)$ attains its minimum value for any integer $m$ in the range $51 \le m \le 61$. Notice the boundary case $m = 61$: for $n = 11$, $2(61) = 122 \le 122$, so the term $f_{11}(m)$ still decreases when moving from $m=60$ to $m=61$ (specifically, $\max(60, 122-60) = 62$ drops to $\max(61, 122-61) = 61$). Thus, 10 terms increase and 10 terms decrease, keeping $S(61) = S(60) = 2495$. We are asked to compute the minimum possible value for $g(14)$. For $n = 14$ (which is in the regime where $m \le n^2 + 1 - m$ for all optimal $m$): $$ g(14) = 14^2 + 1 - m = 197 - m $$ To make $g(14)$ as small as possible, we must select the largest valid $m$ in our optimal range. Taking $m = 61$, we get: $$ g(14) = 197 - 61 = 136 $$

\textbf{Mode A: multiple-choice / true-false / numerical answer} \begin{itemize} \item Exact expected answer: 136 \item Numerical tolerance, if any: None \item Acceptable equivalent forms, units, or rounding conventions: Exact integer only. \item Deterministic verifier: Check if the final parsed numerical response equals 136. \end{itemize}

\begin{msbproblem}

\begin{problemstatement}
An integer-valued function $f$ is called tenuous if $f(x)+f(y)>x^{2}$ for all positive integers $x$ and $y$. Let $g$ be a tenuous function such that $g(1)+g(2)+\cdots+g(20)$ is as small as possible. Compute the minimum possible value for $g(14)$.
\end{problemstatement}

\begin{answerkey}
The minimum possible value for $g(14)$ is 136.

\textbf{Solution:}
Let $m = \min_{y \in \mathbb{Z}^+} g(y)$ be the global minimum of the function $g$. 
Since $g$ is a tenuous function, for any positive integers $x$ and $y$, we are given:
$$ g(x) + g(y) > x^2 $$
Because $g$ is an integer-valued function, the strict inequality can be written as:
$$ g(x) + g(y) \ge x^2 + 1 $$
By choosing $y$ such that $g(y) = m$, we obtain:
$$ g(x) + m \ge x^2 + 1 \implies g(x) \ge x^2 + 1 - m $$
By the definition of $m$, it is also always true that $g(x) \ge m$. Therefore, for all positive integers $x$:
$$ g(x) \ge \max(m, \ x^2 + 1 - m) $$

We want to minimize the sum $\sum_{n=1}^{20} g(n)$. To achieve the minimum possible sum, $g(n)$ must reach its lower bound for all $1 \le n \le 20$. Let $S(m)$ be this minimum possible sum:
$$ S(m) = \sum_{n=1}^{20} \max(m, \ n^2 + 1 - m) $$
We need to find the integer $m$ that minimizes $S(m)$. Let's analyze how the terms change as $m$ increases.
For a fixed $n$, let $f_n(m) = \max(m, n^2 + 1 - m)$. 
If we increase $m$ by 1 (from $m$ to $m+1$):
- $f_n(m)$ increases by 1 if $m > n^2 + 1 - m$, which means $2m > n^2 + 1$.
- $f_n(m)$ decreases by 1 if $m \le n^2 + 1 - m$, which means $2m \le n^2 + 1$.

To minimize $S(m)$, the number of terms that increase must not exceed the number of terms that decrease. Since there are 20 terms in total, we can afford at most 10 terms to increase. This means the condition $2m > n^2 + 1$ can hold for at most the first 10 values of $n$ ($n=1, 2, \dots, 10$).
For $n = 11$, we must have:
$$ 2m \le 11^2 + 1 = 122 \implies m \le 61 $$
At the same time, we need at least 10 terms to increase to stop the sum from decreasing when $m$ goes backwards. This requires the condition to hold for $n=10$:
$$ 2m > 10^2 + 1 = 101 \implies m \ge 51 $$
Therefore, $S(m)$ attains its minimum value for any integer $m$ in the range $51 \le m \le 61$. 

Notice the boundary case $m = 61$: for $n = 11$, $2(61) = 122 \le 122$, so the term $f_{11}(m)$ still decreases when moving from $m=60$ to $m=61$ (specifically, $\max(60, 122-60) = 62$ drops to $\max(61, 122-61) = 61$). Thus, 10 terms increase and 10 terms decrease, keeping $S(61) = S(60) = 2495$.

We are asked to compute the minimum possible value for $g(14)$. For $n = 14$ (which is in the regime where $m \le n^2 + 1 - m$ for all optimal $m$):
$$ g(14) = 14^2 + 1 - m = 197 - m $$
To make $g(14)$ as small as possible, we must select the largest valid $m$ in our optimal range. Taking $m = 61$, we get:
$$ g(14) = 197 - 61 = 136 $$
\end{answerkey}

\begin{gradingscheme}
\textbf{Mode A: multiple-choice / true-false / numerical answer}
\begin{itemize}
\item Exact expected answer: 136
\item Numerical tolerance, if any: None
\item Acceptable equivalent forms, units, or rounding conventions: Exact integer only.
\item Deterministic verifier: Check if the final parsed numerical response equals 136.
\end{itemize}
\end{gradingscheme}

🧩 Failure / success-mode synthesis — how the models miss (and where they don’t)

Synthesized from the sweep (73 runs) + captured traces. INFORMATION for a human researcher to adjudicate — where a call isn’t objectively falsifiable it’s left as an open question (#111 / #102).

  • Partial success: the oracle 136 was reached in 22/73 runs (30%) — by claude-haiku-4-5, claude-opus-4-6, claude-opus-4-7, claude-sonnet-4-6, gpt-5.4, gpt-5.5. So it is reachable, just not reliably; the rest of the field lands elsewhere (see common wrong answers).
  • Common wrong answers: 196×18 · None×11 · 195×4 · 137×3. The most-given non-oracle answer is 196 (18 runs) — a clustered wrong answer usually means one shared misstep, not random error.
  • The shared misstep (verbatim): the consensus answer 136 comes from this reading —
    … on is actually achievable. If I try to make $g(14)$ smaller than 136, I'd need $g(14) \leq 135$. However, the constraint that $g(14) \geq 197 - M = 136$ forces $g(14) \geq 136$, creating a contradiction. Similarly, increasing $M$ beyond 61 only makes the total sum larger. So the minimum value for $g(14)$ is indeed 136.
  • Web-search rescue: with web on, the oracle was hit 3/13 times — search shifted the field toward the oracle, a possible contamination / look-up-ability signal.
  • Red flag — a small model reached the oracle: claude-haiku-4-5 hit 136. The bar is to fail frontier models; a small/cheap model solving it is a sign the problem may be too easy or look-up-able.

Open questions (for a human — not verdicts):

  • ❓ Web-search reaching the oracle when offline runs don’t — is the answer look-up-able (contamination risk), or did search just supply a missing fact?
  • ❓ A small model reached the oracle — does this problem still stump the frontier models it is meant to, or is it too easy / contaminated?

Let \(a_i=g(i)\). Since \(g\) is integer-valued and tenuous, \[ g(x)+g(y)>x^2 \] for all positive integers \(x,y\). Applying this both ways gives, for \(i\le j\), \[ a_i+a_j \ge j^2+1. \] Pair the first 20 terms as \[ (a_1,a_{20}), (a_2,a_{19}),\dots,(a_{10},a_{11}). \] Then \[ a_{21-j}+a_j\ge j^2+1 \qquad (j=11,\dots,20), \] so \[ \sum_{i=1}^{20} a_i \ge \sum_{j=11}^{20}(j^2+1). \] For the sum to be minimal, equality must hold in each of these 10 inequalities: \[ a_{21-j}+a_j=j^2+1. \] Now for any \(j,k\in\{11,\dots,20\}\), the constraint involving \(a_{21-j}\) and \(a_k\) gives \[ a_{21-j}+a_k\ge k^2+1. \] Using \(a_{21-j}=j^2+1-a_j\), we get \[ a_k-(k^2+1)\ge a_j-(j^2+1). \] Since \(j,k\) are arbitrary, all quantities \(a_j-(j^2+1)\) are equal. Thus for some integer \(L\), \[ a_i=L \quad (1\le i\le 10), \qquad a_j=j^2+1-L \quad (11\le j\le 20). \] The constraint at \(j=11\) gives \[ 2a_{11}\ge 11^2+1=122. \] But \(a_{11}=122-L\), so \[ 2(122-L)\ge 122 \implies L\le 61. \] Therefore \[ g(14)=14^2+1-L=197-L\ge 197-61=136. \] This value is attainable, for example by taking \(L=61\). Hence the minimum possible value is Final answer: 136

Let \(m=\min\{g(n): n\in \mathbb Z_{>0}\}\). This minimum exists since \(2g(n)>n^2\), so \(g(n)\to\infty\). For any \(x\), choosing \(y\) with \(g(y)=m\) gives \[ g(x)+m>x^2, \] so, since \(g(x)\) is an integer, \[ g(x)\ge x^2-m+1. \] Also \(g(x)\ge m\). Hence \[ g(x)\ge \max(m,x^2-m+1). \] Conversely, for any integer \(m\ge1\), the function \[ g_m(x)=\max(m,x^2-m+1) \] is tenuous. Thus we minimize \[ S(m)=\sum_{x=1}^{20}\max(m,x^2-m+1). \] For a given \(m\), the term equals \(m\) when \(x^2+1\le 2m\). Let \[ k=\left\lfloor \sqrt{2m-1}\right\rfloor. \] Then for \(x\le k\) the term is \(m\), and for \(x>k\) it is \(x^2-m+1\). Therefore \(S(m)\) is decreasing while \(k<10\), constant while \(k=10\), and increasing while \(k>10\). The case \(k=10\) occurs when \[ 10^2+1\le 2m < 11^2+1, \] so \[ 51\le m\le 60. \] Thus every minimizing function has \(m\in\{51,\dots,60\}\). For \(x=14\), since \(14>10\), \[ g(14)=14^2-m+1=197-m. \] To make \(g(14)\) as small as possible, take \(m=60\), giving \[ g(14)=197-60=137. \] Final answer: \(137\)

Let \(a=\min f(n)\). This minimum exists since \(f(1)+f(y)>1\) gives a lower bound on all \(f(y)\). For a tenuous \(f\), choosing \(y\) with \(f(y)=a\), \[ f(x)+a>x^2 \implies f(x)\ge x^2-a+1. \] Also \(f(x)\ge a\), so \[ f(x)\ge \max(a,x^2-a+1). \] Thus for fixed \(a\), the smallest possible value of the sum is \[ S(a)=\sum_{n=1}^{20}\max(a,n^2-a+1). \] Now \[ \max(a,n^2-a+1)=\frac{n^2+1}{2}+\left|a-\frac{n^2+1}{2}\right|. \] So minimizing \(S(a)\) is equivalent to minimizing the sum of absolute deviations from the \(20\) numbers \[ \frac{n^2+1}{2},\qquad n=1,\dots,20. \] For \(20\) numbers, any median between the 10th and 11th values minimizes this sum. These are \[ \frac{10^2+1}{2}=50.5,\qquad \frac{11^2+1}{2}=61. \] Since \(a\) is an integer, the minimizing values are \[ a=51,52,\dots,61. \] For \(n=14\), \[ g(14)=\max(a,14^2-a+1)=\max(a,197-a). \] For \(a\in[51,61]\), this equals \(197-a\), so it is minimized when \(a=61\): \[ g(14)=197-61=136. \] Final answer: \(\boxed{136}\)

Since \(f\) is integer-valued and the condition holds for all ordered pairs, for positive integers \(i,j\le 20\), \[ f(i)+f(j)>\max(i^2,j^2), \] so \[ f(i)+f(j)\ge \max(i^2,j^2)+1. \] Pair the numbers \(1,\dots,20\) as \[ (1,11),(2,12),\dots,(10,20). \] Then \[ \sum_{i=1}^{20} g(i) =\sum_{k=1}^{10}\bigl(g(k)+g(k+10)\bigr) \ge \sum_{k=1}^{10}\bigl((k+10)^2+1\bigr). \] Thus the minimum possible sum is at least \[ \sum_{j=11}^{20}(j^2+1)=2495. \] This lower bound is achievable: take \[ g(1)=\cdots=g(10)=61,\qquad g(j)=j^2-60\quad(11\le j\le 20), \] and extend, for example, by \(g(n)=n^2\) for \(n>20\). This is tenuous and has sum \(2495\). So for an optimal \(g\), equality must hold in every paired inequality: \[ g(k)+g(k+10)=(k+10)^2+1. \] Let \(g(1)=\cdots=g(10)=t\). Indeed, comparing the constraint involving \(g(i)\) and \(g(k+10)\) forces all first ten values to be equal. Then \[ g(14)=14^2+1-t=197-t. \] Also \[ g(11)=122-t, \] and the condition \(2g(11)>11^2=121\) gives \[ 122-t\ge 61 \implies t\le 61. \] Therefore \[ g(14)=197-t\ge 197-61=136. \] This value is achieved by the construction above. Final answer: 136

Let \(m\) be the minimum value of a tenuous function \(f\). Such a minimum exists because \(f(1)+f(y)>1\) bounds all \(f(y)\) below. For all positive integers \(x\), \[ f(x)+m>x^2. \] Since \(f\) is integer-valued, \[ f(x)+m\ge x^2+1, \] so \[ f(x)\ge x^2+1-m. \] Also \(f(x)\ge m\). Therefore, for fixed \(m\), \[ f(x)\ge \max(m,x^2+1-m). \] This lower bound is attainable for all \(x\), so minimizing \[ f(1)+\cdots+f(20) \] amounts to minimizing \[ S(m)=\sum_{x=1}^{20}\max(m,x^2+1-m). \] Now \[ \max(m,x^2+1-m)=\frac{x^2+1}{2}+\left|m-\frac{x^2+1}{2}\right|. \] Thus \(S(m)\) is minimized when \(m\) is a median of the numbers \[ \frac{x^2+1}{2},\qquad x=1,\dots,20. \] The 10th and 11th values are \[ \frac{10^2+1}{2}=\frac{101}{2}=50.5, \qquad \frac{11^2+1}{2}=61. \] So the integer minimizers are \[ m=51,52,\dots,61. \] For \(x=14\), \[ g(14)=\max(m,14^2+1-m)=\max(m,197-m). \] For \(m\in\{51,\dots,61\}\), we have \(197-m>m\), so \[ g(14)=197-m. \] This is minimized by taking \(m=61\), giving \[ g(14)=197-61=136. \] Final answer: \(\boxed{136}\)

Let \(m=\min_{n\ge 1} f(n)\), which exists since \(f(n)+f(n)>n^2\) implies \(f(n)\to\infty\). For every positive integer \(x\), \[ f(x)+m>x^2, \] so since \(f(x)\) is integer, \[ f(x)\ge x^2-m+1. \] Also \(f(x)\ge m\), hence \[ f(x)\ge \max(m,x^2-m+1). \] Conversely, for any integer \(m\ge 1\), the function \[ f(x)=\max(m,x^2-m+1) \] is tenuous and has minimum value \(m\). Thus minimizing \[ f(1)+\cdots+f(20) \] amounts to minimizing \[ S(m)=\sum_{x=1}^{20}\max(m,x^2-m+1). \] Now \[ \max(m,x^2-m+1)=\frac{x^2+1}{2}+\left|m-\frac{x^2+1}{2}\right|. \] So \(S(m)\) is minimized when \(m\) is a median of the 20 numbers \[ \frac{x^2+1}{2},\qquad x=1,\dots,20. \] The middle two are \[ \frac{10^2+1}{2}=\frac{101}{2}=50.5,\qquad \frac{11^2+1}{2}=61. \] Hence the integer minimizers are \[ m=51,52,\dots,61. \] For such an optimal function, \[ g(14)=\max(m,14^2-m+1)=\max(m,197-m). \] For \(51\le m\le 61\), we have \(197-m>m\), so \[ g(14)=197-m. \] This is minimized by taking \(m=61\), giving \[ g(14)=197-61=136. \] Final answer: 136

Since \(f\) is integer-valued, the condition \[ f(x)+f(y)>x^2 \] is equivalent to \[ f(x)+f(y)\ge x^2+1. \] Let \(L=\min f(n)\). This minimum exists because \(f\) is bounded below. Then for every \(n\), \[ f(n)\ge L \] and using a value \(y\) with \(f(y)=L\), \[ f(n)+L\ge n^2+1, \] so \[ f(n)\ge \max\{L,n^2+1-L\}. \] Thus for a fixed \(L\), the smallest possible value of \[ f(1)+\cdots+f(20) \] is \[ S(L)=\sum_{n=1}^{20}\max\{L,n^2+1-L\}. \] Now \[ \max\{L,n^2+1-L\}=\frac{n^2+1}{2}+\left|L-\frac{n^2+1}{2}\right|. \] So \(S(L)\) is minimized when \(L\) is a median of the numbers \[ \frac{n^2+1}{2},\qquad n=1,\dots,20. \] The 10th and 11th such values are \[ \frac{10^2+1}{2}=\frac{101}{2}=50.5, \qquad \frac{11^2+1}{2}=61. \] Therefore the integer minimizers are \[ L=51,52,\dots,61. \] For \(n=14\), \[ g(14)\ge \max\{L,14^2+1-L\} =\max\{L,197-L\}. \] Since \(L\le 61\), this equals \(197-L\), which is minimized by taking \(L=61\). Hence \[ g(14)=197-61=136. \] Final answer: 136

For integers \(i \le j\), tenuousness implies (using \(x=j,y=i\)): \[ g(i)+g(j)>j^2 \implies g(i)+g(j)\ge j^2+1. \] For every \(i=1,\dots,10\) and \(j=11,\dots,20\), \[ g(i)+g(j)\ge j^2+1. \] Summing these \(100\) inequalities gives \[ 10\sum_{n=1}^{20} g(n)\ge 10\sum_{j=11}^{20}(j^2+1), \] so \[ \sum_{n=1}^{20} g(n)\ge \sum_{j=11}^{20}(j^2+1). \] Now \[ \sum_{j=11}^{20}j^2=2485, \] so the minimum possible sum is at least \[ 2485+10=2495. \] This bound is attainable, for example by defining \[ g(n)= \begin{cases} 61, & 1\le n\le 11,\\ n^2-60, & n\ge 12. \end{cases} \] One checks this is tenuous, and its first \(20\) values sum to \(2495\). Now suppose \(g\) has this minimum sum. Then all \(100\) inequalities above must be equalities. Thus for \(i=1,\dots,10\) and \(j=11,\dots,20\), \[ g(i)+g(j)=j^2+1. \] So \(g(1)=\cdots=g(10)=M\), and in particular \[ g(14)=14^2+1-M=197-M. \] Also, \[ g(11)=11^2+1-M=122-M. \] Since \(g(11)+g(11)>11^2\), we need \[ 2g(11)\ge 122, \] so \[ 2(122-M)\ge 122 \implies M\le 61. \] Therefore \[ g(14)=197-M\ge 197-61=136. \] The construction above has \(g(14)=14^2-60=136\), so this is attainable. Final answer: \(\boxed{136}\)

For a tenuous function, we have \[ f(x)+f(y)>x^2 \qquad \text{for all positive integers }x,y. \] We want a tenuous integer-valued function \(g\) minimizing \[ g(1)+g(2)+\cdots+g(20), \] and then find the minimum possible value of \(g(14)\). ## Step 1: Convert the condition into lower bounds on each \(g(y)\) Fix \(y\). Since the condition must hold for every \(x\), \[ g(y) > x^2 - g(x)\qquad \text{for all }x. \] Because \(g(y)\) is an integer, this means \[ g(y)\ge \max_x (x^2-g(x)+1). \] Let \[ M=\max_x (x^2-g(x)+1). \] Then every \(g(y)\ge M\). In particular, if \(m=\min_n g(n)\), choose \(y\) with \(g(y)=m\). Then for every \(x\), \[ g(x)+m>x^2, \] so since values are integers, \[ g(x)\ge x^2-m+1. \] Also taking minimum over \(x\), we get \(m\ge x^2-m+1\) for all \(x\), hence \[ 2m\ge x^2+1 \qquad \text{for all }x. \] For \(x=20\), \[ 2m\ge 401 \implies m\ge 201. \] So every \(g(n)\ge 201\). Then from \(g(x)+m>x^2\) with \(m=201\) as small as possible, we get \[ g(x)\ge x^2-200. \] Combining with \(g(x)\ge 201\), \[ g(x)\ge \max(201,\;x^2-200). \] Thus any tenuous function satisfies \[ g(x)\ge \max(201,\;x^2-200). \] ## Step 2: Show this bound is attainable Define \[ g(x)=\max(201,\;x^2-200). \] Then for any positive integers \(x,y\), we have \(g(y)\ge 201\), so \[ g(x)+g(y)\ge (x^2-200)+201=x^2+1>x^2. \] Thus this \(g\) is tenuous. Since each \(g(x)\) is as small as allowed independently, this choice minimizes \(\sum_{k=1}^{20} g(k)\). ## Step 3: Compute \(g(14)\) \[ 14^2-200=196-200=-4, \] so \[ g(14)=\max(201,-4)=201. \] Final answer: 201

For a tenuous function, we have \[ f(x)+f(y)>x^2 \qquad \text{for all positive integers }x,y. \] We want a tenuous integer-valued function \(g\) minimizing \[ g(1)+g(2)+\cdots+g(20), \] and then find the minimum possible value of \(g(14)\). ## Step 1: Convert the condition into lower bounds on each \(g(y)\) Fix \(x\). Then for every \(y\), \[ g(y) > x^2 - g(x). \] Since this must hold for all \(x\), we get \[ g(y) \ge \max_x (x^2-g(x)+1). \] A cleaner way is to use symmetry: for every pair \(x,y\), \[ g(x)+g(y)\ge x^2+1 \] since both sides are integers and \(g(x)+g(y)>x^2\). Also swapping \(x,y\), \[ g(x)+g(y)\ge y^2+1. \] Hence \[ g(x)+g(y)\ge \max(x^2,y^2)+1. \] In particular, if \(y\ge x\), then \[ g(x)+g(y)\ge y^2+1. \] ## Step 2: Find the pointwise smallest possible values Let \[ m=\min_{1\le k\le 20} g(k). \] Take \(x\) such that \(g(x)=m\). Then for any \(y\), \[ m+g(y)>y^2, \] so since values are integers, \[ g(y)\ge y^2-m+1. \] Thus every \(g(y)\) is bounded below by \(y^2-m+1\), and equality for all \(y\) would minimize the sum. But then at \(y=x\), \[ m=g(x)\ge x^2-m+1, \] so \[ 2m\ge x^2+1. \] To make the total sum as small as possible, we want \(m\) as small as possible, and we should choose \(x=1\) (smallest square). Then \[ 2m\ge 2 \implies m\ge 1. \] So the smallest possible minimum value is \(m=1\). Then the pointwise lower bound becomes \[ g(y)\ge y^2. \] And this is achievable by taking \[ g(y)=y^2 \quad \text{for all } y. \] Indeed, \[ g(x)+g(y)=x^2+y^2>x^2 \] for all positive integers \(x,y\), so this is tenuous. Since each \(g(y)\) is at least \(y^2\), this choice minimizes the sum \(g(1)+\cdots+g(20)\). ## Step 3: Read off \(g(14)\) Thus in any sum-minimizing tenuous function, \[ g(14)=14^2=196. \] Final answer: 196

For a tenuous function, we have \[ f(x)+f(y)>x^2 \qquad \text{for all positive integers }x,y. \] We want a tenuous integer-valued function \(g\) minimizing \[ g(1)+g(2)+\cdots+g(20), \] and then find the minimum possible value of \(g(14)\). ## Step 1: Rewrite the condition Since the condition holds for all \(y\), for fixed \(x\) we must have \[ g(y) > x^2 - g(x)\qquad \text{for all }y. \] So if \(m=\min_{n\ge1} g(n)\), then for every \(x\), \[ g(x)+m>x^2. \] Because values are integers, this means \[ g(x)\ge x^2-m+1. \] Also, since \(m\) is the minimum value, there is some \(t\) with \(g(t)=m\). Then plugging \(x=t\) and \(y=t\), \[ 2m>t^2. \] So \(m\) must satisfy \[ m>\frac{t^2}{2} \] for at least one positive integer \(t\). To minimize the sum on \(1,\dots,20\), we should make \(m\) as small as possible. The smallest possible minimum is achieved with \(t=1\), giving \[ 2m>1 \implies m\ge1. \] So the smallest possible minimum value is \(m=1\). ## Step 2: Best possible lower bounds when \(m=1\) If \(m=1\), then for every \(x\), \[ g(x)+1>x^2. \] Since \(g(x)\) is an integer, \[ g(x)\ge x^2. \] Thus for \(x=1,\dots,20\), \[ g(x)\ge x^2. \] Hence \[ g(1)+\cdots+g(20)\ge 1^2+2^2+\cdots+20^2. \] This lower bound is attained by taking \[ g(x)=x^2 \quad \text{for all }x. \] Indeed, \[ g(x)+g(y)=x^2+y^2>x^2 \] for all positive integers \(x,y\), so this is tenuous. Therefore the sum is minimized exactly by \(g(x)=x^2\) for \(x=1,\dots,20\) (and in particular this choice works globally). ## Step 3: Compute \(g(14)\) So in any sum-minimizing tenuous function, \[ g(14)=14^2=196. \] Final answer: 196

Let \[ m=\min_{n\ge 1} g(n). \] Since \(g\) is integer-valued and for all \(y\), \[ g(1)+g(y)>1, \] the values of \(g\) are bounded below, so this minimum exists. # Finding the Minimum Value of g(14) ## Understanding the Constraint A tenuous function satisfies $f(x) + f(y) > x^2$ for all positive integers $x$ and $y$. Since this must hold for **all** positive integers $y$, the most restrictive case is when $f(y)$ is minimized. Let me think about this differently. For fixed $x$, the condition $f(x) + f(y) > x^2$ must hold for all $y$. The binding constraint comes from the smallest value of $f(y)$. Similarly, for fixed $y$, $f(x) + f(y) > x^2$ must hold for all $x$. The binding constraint comes from the largest $x^2$. **Key insight:** Setting $y$ to minimize $f(y)$: if $m = \min_y f(y)$, then $f(x) + m > x^2$, so $f(x) > x^2 - m$ for all $x$, meaning $f(x) \geq x^2 - m + 1$. Also, setting $x$ to maximize $x^2$: for any $y$, $f(x) + f(y) > x^2$ for all $x$. In particular, for any $y$, $f(y) > x^2 - f(x)$ for all $x$. ## Optimizing the Sum We want to minimize $S = \sum_{x=1}^{20} g(x)$. Since $g(x) \geq x^2 - m + 1$ where $m = \min_{y \geq 1} g(y)$, and the minimum could be achieved at some point in $\{1, \ldots, 20\}$ or outside. **If the minimum is achieved at some $x_0 \in \{1, \ldots, 20\}$:** Then $m = g(x_0) \geq x_0^2 - m + 1$, giving $m \geq \lceil(x_0^2+1)/2\rceil$. **For values outside {1,...,20}:** We can set $g(y)$ to be very large for $y > 20$, so the minimum $m$ is achieved within $\{1,...,20\}$. So $g(x) \geq x^2 - m + 1$ for each $x$, and $m = \min g(x)$ over $\{1,...,20\}$. The sum is $S \geq \sum_{x=1}^{20}(x^2 - m + 1) = \sum x^2 - 20m + 20 = 2870 - 20m + 20 = 2890 - 20m$. To minimize $S$, we want $m$ as large as possible. But $m$ is the minimum of $g$, and $g(x) \geq x^2 - m + 1$. The minimum of $x^2 - m + 1$ over $x \in \{1,...,20\}$ is at $x=1$: $1 - m + 1 = 2 - m$. So $m \geq 2 - m + 1$... wait: $m = \min g(x_0) \geq x_0^2 - m + 1$ at the minimizing point. The minimum of $g$ is most easily achieved at $x_0 = 1$, giving $m \geq 2 - m$, so $m \geq 1$. But we want $m$ large to minimize $S$. We can set some values of $g$ larger than the lower bound. The optimal strategy: set $g(x) = x^2 - m + 1$ for all $x$ (achieving equality), then $m = g(1) = 2 - m$, so $m = 1$. But we could raise $g$ at small values and lower $m$... Actually, to maximize $m$, we should consider: we can set $g(x) = x^2 - m + 1$ except raise $g$ at the point achieving the minimum. If we raise $g(1)$, then the minimum shifts. Let me reconsider. We want to minimize $S$, so set $g(x) = x^2 - m + 1$ (integer-valued). The constraint is $m \leq g(x)$ for all $x$, so $m \leq 2 - m$, giving $m \leq 1$. With $m = 1$: $g(x) = x^2$ for all $x$, and $S = 2870$. Can we do better by not having the minimum inside $\{1,...,20\}$? # Finding the Minimum Value of g(14) ## Understanding the Constraint A tenuous function satisfies $f(x) + f(y) > x^2$ for all positive integers $x$ and $y$. Since this must hold for **all** positive integers $y$, the most restrictive case is when $f(y)$ is minimized. Let me think about this differently. For fixed $x$, we need $f(y) > x^2 - f(x)$ for all positive integers $y$. This means: $$\min_y f(y) > x^2 - f(x) \quad \text{for all } x$$ Let $m = \min_{y \geq 1} f(y)$. Then we need: $$m + f(x) > x^2 \quad \text{for all } x \geq 1$$ $$f(x) > x^2 - m \quad \text{for all } x \geq 1$$ Since $f$ is integer-valued: $f(x) \geq x^2 - m + 1$ for all $x \geq 1$. Also, $m$ itself must satisfy $m \geq x^2 - m + 1$ for the $x$ values where $f$ achieves its minimum... but actually $m$ must satisfy $m \geq 1 - m + 1$ at minimum (from $x=1$), but more importantly, $m$ must be consistent. So $m \geq 2 - m$, meaning $m \geq 1$. ## Minimizing the Sum We want to minimize $\sum_{x=1}^{20} g(x)$ where $g(x) \geq x^2 - m + 1$ for all $x$, and $m = \min_{x} g(x)$. To minimize the sum, set $g(x) = x^2 - m + 1$ for most values, but we can set $g(x) = m$ whenever $m > x^2 - m + 1$, i.e., when $x^2 < 2m - 1$, i.e., $x \leq \lfloor\sqrt{2m-2}\rfloor$. Also, the condition must hold symmetrically: $f(y) + f(x) > y^2$ for all $x, y$. We need $f(x) \geq x^2 - m + 1$ for all $x$. To minimize the sum, we want $f(x) = x^2 - m + 1$ for all $x$ (taking equality). The minimum of $f(x) = x^2 - m + 1$ over positive integers occurs at $x = 1$: $f(1) = 1 - m + 1 = 2 - m$. But we said $m = \min f(x) = 2 - m$, so $m = 2 - m$, giving $m = 1$. So $f(x) = x^2 - 1 + 1 = x^2$. But we can do better by not requiring equality everywhere. ## Better Approach To minimize $\sum_{x=1}^{20} f(x)$, we want $f$ as small as possible. The constraint is $f(x) + f(y) > x^2$ for all $x, y$. For each $x$: $f(x) > x^2 - f(y)$ for all $y$, so $f(x) \geq x^2 - \min_y f(y) + 1$. Let $m = \min_y f(y)$. Then $f(x) \geq x^2 - m + 1$. To minimize the sum, set $f(x) = x^2 - m + 1$ for large $x$ (where $x^2 - m + 1 > m$, i.e., $x^2 > 2m - 1$), and $f(x) = m$ for small $x$. The crossover: $x^2 - m + 1 \leq m \Rightarrow x^2 \leq 2m - 1$. So for $x \leq \lfloor\sqrt{2m-1}\rfloor$, set $f(x) = m$; for larger $x$, set $f(x) = x^2 - m + 1$. Let $k = \lfloor\sqrt{2m-1}\rfloor$. ## Setting Up the Problem We need $f(x) + f(y) > x^2$ for all positive integers $x$ and $y$. For fixed $x$, this must hold for all positive integers $y$, including the $y$ that minimizes $f(y)$. Let $m = \min_{y \geq 1} f(y)$. Then we need: $f(x) + m > x^2$, so $f(x) > x^2 - m$, meaning $f(x) \geq x^2 - m + 1$ (since integer-valued). Also, the condition must hold symmetrically: $f(y) + f(x) > y^2$ for all $x, y$. So for each $x$: $f(x) \geq x^2 - m + 1$ where $m = \min f$. ## Finding the Minimum Sum Let's say the minimum value of $f$ is achieved at some point $y_0$, so $m = f(y_0)$. For all $x$: $f(x) \geq x^2 - m + 1$. In particular, $m = f(y_0) \geq y_0^2 - m + 1$, so $2m \geq y_0^2 + 1$, meaning $m \geq \lceil(y_0^2 + 1)/2\rceil$. To minimize the sum $\sum_{x=1}^{20} f(x)$, we want $f(x)$ as small as possible: $$f(x) = x^2 - m + 1$$ The sum becomes: $$S = \sum_{x=1}^{20}(x^2 - m + 1) = \sum_{x=1}^{20} x^2 - 20m + 20$$ To minimize $S$, we want to maximize $m$. But $m$ is the minimum of $f$, and $f(x) = x^2 - m + 1$, so the minimum of $f$ over $x = 1, \ldots, 20$ occurs at $x = 1$: $f(1) = 1 - m + 1 = 2 - m$. So $m = f(y_0) = 2 - m$ (if minimum is at $x=1$), giving $2m = 2$, $m = 1$. Wait, let me reconsider. If $f(x) = x^2 - m + 1$ for all $x$, then the minimum of $f$ is at $x=1$: $f(1) = 2 - m$. For consistency, $m = 2 - m$, so $m = 1$. With $m = 1$: $f(x) = x^2$. Check: $f(x) + f(y) = x^2 + y^2 > x^2$? This requires $y^2 > 0$, which holds for positive integers. ✓ But can we do better? What if we allow $f$ to not follow the pattern $x^2 - m + 1$ exactly? We could set $f(x) = x^2 - m + 1$ for $x \geq 2$ and set $f(1)$ differently. The constraint is $f(x) + f(y) > x^2$ for all $x, y$. The binding constraint for large $x$ is when $y$ achieves the minimum. If we lower $f(1)$ below $2-m$, then $m$ decreases, which forces all $f(x)$ to increase. Actually, let's try: set $f(1) = c$ (small), and $f(x) = x^2 - c + 1$ for $x \geq 2$. Check constraint: $f(x) + f(y) > x^2$. - $x \geq 2, y = 1$: $(x^2 - c + 1) + c = x^2 + 1 > x^2$ ✓ - $x = 1, y \geq 2$: $c + (y^2 - c + 1) = y^2 + 1 > 1$ ✓ - $x \geq 2, y \geq 2$: $(x^2 - c + 1) + (y^2 - c + 1) > x^2 \Rightarrow y^2 - 2c + 2 > 0 \Rightarrow c < (y^2 + 2)/2$. For $y=2$: $c < 3$, so $c \leq 2$. With $c = 2$: $f(1) = 2$, $f(x) = x^2 - 1$ for $x \geq 2$. Sum $= 2 + \sum_{x=2}^{20}(x^2-1) = 2 + \sum_{x=2}^{20} x^2 - 19$. This is less than $\sum_{x=1}^{20} x^2$ by $1 - 2 + 19 - 19 = -1$... let me just compute $g(14)$. $$g(14) = 14^2 - 1 = 196 - 1 = \boxed{195}$$ Final answer: 195

## Setting Up the Problem We need $f(x) + f(y) > x^2$ for all positive integers $x, y$. For fixed $x$, this must hold for all positive integers $y$, including $y$ that minimizes $f(y)$. Let $m = \min_{y \geq 1} f(y)$. The condition requires: $f(x) + m > x^2$, so $f(x) > x^2 - m$, meaning $f(x) \geq x^2 - m + 1$. Also, the condition must hold symmetrically: $f(y) + f(x) > y^2$ for all $x, y$, so $f(x) \geq y^2 - f(y) + 1$ for all $x, y$. **Key constraint:** For all $x, y$: $f(x) + f(y) > x^2$ AND $f(x) + f(y) > y^2$. So $f(x) + f(y) > \max(x^2, y^2)$ for all $x, y$. For any $x$: taking $y = x$: $2f(x) > x^2$, so $f(x) \geq \lfloor x^2/2 \rfloor + 1$. For $x, y$ with $x > y$: $f(x) + f(y) > x^2$. ## Minimizing the Sum Let $m = \min_y f(y)$. Then $f(x) \geq x^2 - m + 1$ for all $x$. To minimize $\sum_{x=1}^{20} f(x)$, we want $m$ as large as possible and $f(x)$ as small as possible. The minimum value $m$ is achieved at some $y^*$. For that $y^*$: $f(y^*) = m$, and we need $f(x) + m > x^2$ for all $x$, so $f(x) \geq x^2 - m + 1$. Also $2m > (y^*)^2$, so $m \geq \lfloor (y^*)^2/2 \rfloor + 1$. Set $f(x) = x^2 - m + 1$ for $x \geq$ some threshold, and $f(x) = m$ for small $x$ where $m \geq x^2 - m + 1$, i.e., $2m \geq x^2 + 1$, i.e., $x^2 \leq 2m - 1$. Let's say $f(x) = \max(m, x^2 - m + 1)$. The crossover: $x^2 - m + 1 \leq m$ when $x^2 \leq 2m - 1$. Sum $= \sum_{x=1}^{20} \max(m, x^2 - m + 1)$. For $x \leq k$: $f(x) = m$ (where $k^2 \leq 2m-1 < (k+1)^2$) For $x > k$: $f(x) = x^2 - m + 1$ Sum $= km + \sum_{x=k+1}^{20}(x^2 - m + 1) = km + \sum_{x=k+1}^{20} x^2 - (20-k)m + (20-k)$ $= m(2k - 20) + \sum_{x=k+1}^{20} x^2 + (20-k)$ To minimize over $m$: coefficient of $m$ is $2k - 20$. If $k < 10$, coefficient is negative, so increase $m$. If $k > 10$, coefficient is positive, decrease $m$. Optimal at $k = 10$. With $k = 10$: $10^2 \leq 2m - 1$, so $m \geq 51$ (since $100 \leq 2m-1$ gives $m \geq 50.5$, so $m \geq 51$). Check: $11^2 = 121 > 2(51)-1 = 101$. ✓ Sum $= 0 \cdot m + \sum_{x=11}^{20} x^2 + 10 = \sum_{x=11}^{20} x^2 + 10$ This is independent of $m$! So we want $m$ as small as possible to allow $g(14)$ to be minimized. With $m = 51$: $f(x) = x^2 - 50$ for $x \geq 11$. $g(14) = 14^2 - 50 = 196 - 50 = 146$. But can we do better? We could set $f(14)$ smaller if we compensate elsewhere, keeping the sum the same and satisfying constraints. The constraint requires $f(14) + f(y) > 196$ for all $y$, so $f(14) > 196 - m = 196 - 51 = 145$, giving $f(14) \geq 146$. Final answer: **146**

## Setting Up the Optimization For a tenuous function, $f(x) + f(y) > x^2$ for all positive integers $x, y$. Let $m = \min_{y \geq 1} f(y)$. The binding constraint for each $x$ is: $$f(x) + m > x^2 \implies f(x) \geq x^2 - m + 1$$ So the minimum achievable value at each point is: $$f(x) = \max(m, x^2 - m + 1)$$ where $f(x) = m$ when $x^2 \leq 2m-1$ (i.e., $x \leq \sqrt{2m-1}$), and $f(x) = x^2 - m + 1$ otherwise. ## Minimizing the Sum Let $k = \lfloor\sqrt{2m-1}\rfloor$ be the number of values where $f(x) = m$. The sum becomes: $$S = km + \sum_{x=k+1}^{20}(x^2 - m + 1) = m(2k-20) + \sum_{x=k+1}^{20}(x^2+1)$$ **For $k = 10$:** The coefficient of $m$ is $2(10)-20 = 0$, so $S$ is independent of $m$: $$S = \sum_{x=11}^{20}(x^2+1) = 2485 + 10 = 2495$$ **For $k = 11$** (valid range: $m \in [61, 72]$): Minimize $m = 61$: $$S = 11(61) + \sum_{x=12}^{20}(x^2-60) = 671 + 1824 = 2495$$ **For $k = 9$** (maximize $m = 50$): $S = 2496 > 2495$. **For $k = 12$** (minimize $m = 73$): $S = 2520 > 2495$. The **minimum sum is 2495**, achievable with $k = 10$ (any $m \in [51,60]$) or $k = 11, m = 61$. ## Finding the Minimum $g(14)$ **Case $k = 10$, $m \in [51, 60]$:** Since $14 > 10$, we get $g(14) = 196 - m + 1 = 197 - m$. To minimize, maximize $m = 60$: $g(14) = 137$. **Case $k = 11$, $m = 61$:** Since $14 > 11$, we get $g(14) = 196 - 61 + 1 = 136$. **Can we achieve $g(14) = 135$?** This requires $f(14) = 135$, which forces $f(y) \geq 62$ for all $y$ (since $f(y) > 196 - 135 = 61$). With minimum $m = 62$ and $k = 11$: $$S = 11(62) + \sum_{x=12}^{20}(x^2 - 61) = 682 + 1815 = 2497 > 2495$$ No configuration with $f(14) \leq 135$ achieves sum = 2495. ## Verification With $k = 11$, $m = 61$: $f(x) = 61$ for $x \leq 11$ and $f(x) = x^2 - 60$ for $x \geq 12$. Check: $f(14) + f(y) \geq 136 + 61 = 197 > 196 = 14^2$ ✓ Final answer: $\boxed{136}$

## Setting Up the Optimization **Tenuous condition:** $g(x) + g(y) > x^2$ for all positive integers $x, y$. For fixed $x$, this must hold for all $y$, including $y$ achieving $M = \min_y g(y)$. Therefore: $$g(x) + M > x^2 \implies g(x) \geq x^2 - M + 1$$ ## Finding the Minimum Sum With transition point $x_0 = \lceil\sqrt{2M-1}\rceil$, the optimal strategy sets: $$g(x) = \begin{cases} M & x < x_0 \\ x^2 - M + 1 & x \geq x_0 \end{cases}$$ The sum becomes $S = M(2x_0 - 22) + T(x_0) + (21 - x_0)$, where $T(x_0) = \sum_{x=x_0}^{20} x^2$. **Testing $x_0 = 11$** (requiring $50.5 < M \leq 61$, i.e., $M \in \{51,\ldots,61\}$): The coefficient of $M$ is $2(11) - 22 = 0$, so the sum is **independent of $M$**: $$S = T(11) + 10 = 2485 + 10 = \mathbf{2495}$$ **Comparing nearby cases:** - $x_0 = 10$: Best sum $= 2496$ (with $M = 50$) - $x_0 = 12$: Best sum $= 2497$ (with $M = 62$) - $x_0 = 9$: Best sum $= 2514$ So the minimum sum is **2495**, achieved with $x_0 = 11$. ## Minimizing $g(14)$ From the tenuous condition: $g(14) \geq 197 - M$. To minimize $g(14)$, maximize $M$. Within the valid range $M \in \{51, \ldots, 61\}$, take $M = 61$. **Verification with $M = 61$:** - $g(x) = 61$ for $x = 1, \ldots, 10$ - $g(x) = x^2 - 60$ for $x = 11, \ldots, 20$ **Check tenuous condition:** - For $x \leq 10$: $g(x) + g(y) \geq 61 + 61 = 122 > 100 \geq x^2$ ✓ - For $x \geq 11$: $g(x) + g(y) \geq (x^2 - 60) + 61 = x^2 + 1 > x^2$ ✓ **Sum:** $10(61) + \sum_{x=11}^{20}(x^2 - 60) = 610 + 2485 - 600 = 2495$ ✓ Therefore: $$g(14) = 14^2 - 60 = 196 - 60 = 136$$ Final answer: $\boxed{136}$

## Setting Up the Optimization **Goal:** Find a tenuous function $g$ minimizing $\sum_{x=1}^{20} g(x)$, then find the minimum possible $g(14)$. **Tenuousness condition:** $g(x) + g(y) > x^2$ for all positive integers $x, y$. ## Finding the Binding Constraint Let $m = \min_{y=1}^{20} g(y)$. For each fixed $x$, the binding constraint is: $$g(x) + m > x^2 \implies g(x) \geq x^2 - m + 1$$ Since $g(x) \geq m$ always holds, we get: $$g(x) \geq \max\!\left(m,\; x^2 - m + 1\right)$$ The crossover from "$g(x) = m$" to "$g(x) = x^2-m+1$" occurs when $m \approx x^2/2$. ## Minimizing the Sum Over $k$ (Crossover Index) If $g(x) = m$ for $x \leq k$ and $g(x) = x^2 - m + 1$ for $x > k$, the sum is: $$S = m(2k - 20) + \sum_{x=k+1}^{20} x^2 + (20-k)$$ **For $k = 10$:** The coefficient of $m$ is zero, so $S = \sum_{x=11}^{20} x^2 + 10 = 2485 + 10 = \mathbf{2495}$ for any valid $m \in \{51,\ldots,60\}$. **For $k = 11$, $m = 61$ (minimum):** $S = 2(61) + \sum_{x=12}^{20} x^2 + 9 = 122 + 2364 + 9 = \mathbf{2495}$. **For $k = 9$:** Max $m = 50$ gives $S = 2596 - 100 = 2496 > 2495$. **For $k = 12$:** Min $m = 73$ gives $S = 292 + 2228 = 2520 > 2495$. The **minimum sum is 2495**, achieved (among other ways) with $k = 11$, $m = 61$. ## Minimizing $g(14)$ **With $k=10$, $m=60$:** $g(14) = 14^2 - 60 + 1 = 137$. **With $k=11$, $m=61$:** $g(14) = 14^2 - 61 + 1 = \mathbf{136}$. **Can we achieve $g(14) \leq 135$?** If $g(14) = 135$, then for all $y$: $g(y) \geq 196 - 135 + 1 = 62$, forcing $m \geq 62$. The minimum sum with $m = 62$ is: $$11(62) + \sum_{x=12}^{20}(x^2 - 61) = 682 + 1815 = 2497 > 2495$$ This exceeds 2495, so $g(14) \leq 135$ is impossible while keeping the sum minimal. ## Verification With $g(x) = 61$ for $x \leq 11$ and $g(x) = x^2 - 60$ for $x \geq 12$: - **Tenuous check ($x=14$):** $136 + 61 = 197 > 196 = 14^2$ ✓ - **Tenuous check ($x=20$):** $340 + 61 = 401 > 400$ ✓ - **Sum:** $11(61) + (84+109+136+165+196+229+264+301+340) = 671 + 1824 = 2495$ ✓ Final answer: $\boxed{136}$

## Setting Up the Optimization **Understanding the constraint:** Since $f(x)+f(y)>x^2$ must hold for **all** positive integers $x$ and $y$, for fixed $x$ the binding constraint comes from the minimum value of $f$. Let $M = \min_{y\geq 1} f(y)$. Then for all $x \in \{1,\ldots,20\}$: $f(x) + M > x^2$, so $f(x) \geq x^2 - M + 1$. Since $f$ takes integer values and $f(x) \geq M$ (by definition of minimum), the optimal choice is: $$f(x) = \max\!\bigl(M,\; x^2 - M + 1\bigr)$$ The crossover occurs where $x^2 - M + 1 = M$, i.e., $x^2 = 2M-1$. ## Computing the Sum for General $k$ Let $k$ = number of indices in $\{1,\ldots,20\}$ where $f(x) = M$ (i.e., $x^2 < 2M-1$). The sum becomes: $$S = k\cdot M + \sum_{x=k+1}^{20}(x^2 - M + 1) = M(2k-20) + \sum_{x=k+1}^{20} x^2 + (20-k)$$ **Constraints on $M$:** For $x \leq k$: need $2M > k^2$, so $M \geq \lceil(k^2+1)/2\rceil$. For $x = k+1$: $f(k+1) \geq M$, so $M \leq \lfloor((k+1)^2+1)/2\rfloor$. ## Optimizing Over $k$ - **$k < 10$:** Coefficient $2k-20 < 0$, so maximize $M$ → $M = \lfloor((k+1)^2+1)/2\rfloor$ - **$k = 10$:** Coefficient $= 0$, so $M$ doesn't affect $S$ - **$k > 10$:** Coefficient $> 0$, so minimize $M$ Computing $S$ for key values: | $k$ | $M$ | $S$ | |-----|------|------| | 8 | 41 | 2514 | | 9 | 50 | 2496 | | **10** | any valid | **2495** | | 11 | 61 | 2495 | | 12 | 73 | 2520 | The minimum sum is $\boxed{2495}$, achieved at $k=10$ (or equivalently $k=11$ with $M=61$). ## Minimizing $g(14)$ At $k = 10$: $M$ ranges from $51$ to $61$, and for $x > 10$: $$f(x) = x^2 - M + 1$$ So $g(14) = 196 - M + 1 = 197 - M$. To **minimize** $g(14)$, we **maximize** $M$. The upper bound comes from $x = 11$: $$M \leq \left\lfloor\frac{121+1}{2}\right\rfloor = 61$$ Setting $M = 61$: $f(1)=\cdots=f(11)=61$ and $f(x)=x^2-60$ for $x\geq 12$. **Verification:** For any $x, y$: - $x \leq 11$: $f(x)+f(y) \geq 61+61=122 > 121 \geq x^2$ ✓ - $x = 14$: $136+61=197 > 196=14^2$ ✓ - $x = 20$: $341+61=402 > 400$ ✓ $$g(14) = 196 - 61 + 1 = \mathbf{136}$$ **Final answer: 136**

## Setting Up the Optimization **Understanding "tenuous":** The condition $f(x)+f(y)>x^2$ must hold for all positive integers $x,y$. **Key observation:** Let $M = \min_{y\geq 1} f(y)$. Then for each $x$, the binding constraint is: $$f(x) + M > x^2 \implies f(x) \geq x^2 - M + 1$$ For values $y > 20$, I can choose $f(y)$ arbitrarily large so they don't restrict values on $\{1,\ldots,20\}$. ## Structural Form of Optimal $g$ Suppose the minimum $M$ is achieved at positions $j = 1, \ldots, k$ (so $g(j) = M$ for $j \leq k$, and $g(x) = x^2 - M + 1$ for $x > k$). **Constraint from the minimum positions:** $g(j)+g(y)>j^2$ with $g(y)\geq M$ gives $2M > k^2$, so: $$M \geq \left\lceil\frac{k^2+1}{2}\right\rceil$$ **Total sum formula:** $$S(k) = kM + \sum_{x=k+1}^{20}(x^2 - M + 1) = M(2k-20) + \sum_{x=k+1}^{20}x^2 + (20-k)$$ where $M = \lceil(k^2+1)/2\rceil$. ## Computing $S(k)$ to Find the Minimum Using $\sum_{x=1}^{20}x^2 = 2870$: | $k$ | $M$ | $S(k)$ | |-----|-----|--------| | 8 | 33 | 2546 | | 9 | 41 | 2514 | | **10** | **51** | **2495** | | **11** | **61** | **2495** | | 12 | 73 | 2520 | | 13 | 85 | 2568 | The **minimum sum is 2495**, achieved at $k=10$ and $k=11$. ## Finding Minimum $g(14)$ **Constraint on $g(14)$:** Since $g(14) + M > 14^2 = 196$, we need $g(14) \geq 197 - M$. To minimize $g(14)$, I want $M$ as large as possible. - **$k=10$:** $M=51$, gives $g(14) = 14^2 - 50 = 146$ - **$k=11$:** $M=61$, gives $g(14) = 14^2 - 60 = 136$ **Verifying $k=11$ achieves sum 2495:** $$g(1)=\cdots=g(11)=61,\quad g(x)=x^2-60 \text{ for } x=12,\ldots,20$$ $$S = 11(61) + \sum_{x=12}^{20}(x^2-60) = 671 + (2364 - 540) = 671 + 1824 = 2495\checkmark$$ **Could $M > 61$ give $g(14) < 136$?** Trying $M=62$ (with the same structure) yields sum $2497 > 2495$. Any adjustment to reduce the sum would force $g(14)\geq 197-M=136$ again by the tenuous constraint. The bound $g(14) \geq 197 - M = 136$ is tight. ## Verification With $k=11$, $M=61$: for all $x,y$, $g(x)+g(y) \geq g(x)+61 > x^2$ ✓ Final answer: $\boxed{136}$

More precisely: - If x is even: x²/2 is an integer, so f(x) ≥ x²/2 + 1 - If x is odd: x²/2 is not an integer, so f(x) ≥ ⌈x²/2⌉ = (x²+1)/2 **Setting y = 1:** For any x: f(x) + f(1) > x² This gives: f(x) > x² - f(1) So: f(x) ≥ x² - f(1) + 1 **Combining constraints:** For each x, we need: - f(x) > x²/2 (from setting y = x) - f(x) ≥ x² - f(1) + 1 (from setting y = 1) The second constraint is stronger when x² - f(1) + 1 > x²/2, i.e., when f(1) < x²/2 + 1. **Minimizing the sum:** To minimize Σg(i), I want each g(x) as small as possible. From f(x) + f(1) > x², we get f(x) ≥ x² - f(1) + 1. Let me try f(1) = c for some value c. Then: - g(x) ≥ x² - c + 1 for all x ≥ 1 - Also g(1) ≥ 1/2 + 1 = 1 (since 2g(1) > 1) So g(1) ≥ 1, meaning c ≥ 1. If g(1) = 1, then g(x) ≥ x² - 1 + 1 = x² for x ≥ 2. But we also need g(x) > x²/2, which is satisfied by g(x) ≥ x². **Testing g(x) = x² for x ≥ 2 and g(1) = 1:** Check: g(x) + g(y) > x²? - If x = 1: g(1) + g(y) = 1 + y² > 1 ✓ - If x ≥ 2: g(x) + g(y) = x² + g(y) ≥ x² + 1 > x² ✓ This works! The sum is: g(1) + Σ(i=2 to 20) i² = 1 + (2² + 3² +... + 20²) Using Σ(i=1 to n) i² = n(n+1)(2n+1)/6: Σ(i=1 to 20) i² = 20(21)(41)/6 = 2870 Σ(i=2 to 20) i² = 2870 - 1 = 2869 Sum = 1 + 2869 = 2870 **Finding g(14):** With g(x) = x² for x ≥ 2: g(14) = 14² = 196 **Verification that we can't do better:** Could we have g(14) < 196? But we also need 2g(2) > 4, so g(2) ≥ 3. The constraint from y = 1 becomes tighter as g(1) increases, making the sum larger. So g(1) = 1 is optimal, forcing g(14) ≥ 196. Final answer: **196**
